The First Argument Against Time and Its Refutation
Imam Razi presents and refutes the first argument against the existence of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument against the existence of time and Imam Razi's refutation, which avoids both the identity of past/present and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Time's parts are intrinsically ordered; no extra time is needed.

This passage introduces the first argument against the reality of time, posed by Aristotelian philosophers. The argument presents a dilemma: if time is a stable entity, past and present collapse; if it is non-stable, infinite regress follows. Imam Razi refutes both horns, showing that time's parts have inherent priority and posteriority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities: if time is stable in essence, the present equals the past; if not stable, then its parts are successive, leading to an infinite regress. Grasp this dichotomy.
2
Analyze the First Horn
If time is stable, why would present and past be identical? The arguer claims that because time is unchanging, all events would occupy the same temporal moment. Reflect on this.
3
Analyze the Second Horn
If time is not stable, then a part of it is present while a previous part is gone, so we would need a second time to measure this succession, leading to regress. Consider this reasoning.
4
Examine Razi's Response
Razi refutes both: stable time does not imply identity (it can coexist in a stable body), and non-stable time's parts are ordered by themselves, not requiring another time. See how each horn is addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الزمان فهو مقدار الحركة عند أرسطاطاليس. فقد احتجوا على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون موجودا بأمور. أولها أنه لو كان موجودا إما أن يكون قار الذات فيكون الحاضر عين الماضى فيكون الحادث اليوم حادثا زمان الطوفان هذا خلف، أو لا يكون قار الذات وحينئذ يقضي العقل بأن جزءا منه كان موجودا ولم يبق الآن فإن جزءا منه حصل الآن، والماضي والآن هو الزمان فيلزم منه وقوع الزمان في الزمان، فلو كان أمرا وجوديا لزم التسلسل وهو محال. أقول: إن كان الزمان قار الذات لا يكون الحاضر عين الماضى بل يكون معا في الجسم الذي هو قار الذات ولا يلزم منه أن يكون جزء منه هو عين الجزء الآخر، وأما إذا كان الزمان غير قار الذات ولم يبق جزء منه عند حصول جزء آخر فلا يلزم منه أن يكون للزمان زمان لأن القبلية والبعدية لأجزاء الزمان لذاتها فيكون جزء مقدما على جزء لا بزمان غيرهما بل بذاتيهما ولا يلزم منه التسلسل.
As for time (al-zamān), it is the measure of motion according to Aristotle. They argued that it cannot be existent by several points. The first: if it were existent, it would either be stable in essence (qārr al-dhāt), in which case the present would be identical to the past, so that today's event would be an event of the time of the Flood—this is a contradiction; or it is not stable in essence, and then reason judges that a part of it existed and does not remain now, for a part of it occurs now, while the past and the now is time, so it follows that time occurs in time; thus if it were an existent thing, infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. I say: If time were stable in essence, the present would not be identical to the past; rather they would be together in the body that is stable in essence, and it would not follow that one part is identical to another part. As for when time is not stable in essence and no part of it remains when another part occurs, it does not follow that time has a time, because priority and posteriority belong to the parts of time by themselves; so one part is prior to another not by another time, but by their essences, and no infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents and answers the first Aristotelian argument against the existence of time. The argument poses a dilemma: if time is a stable entity, past and present would be identical, absurd; if it is non-stable, then its parts are successive, leading to time occurring in time (regress). The response rejects both horns: stable time does not imply identity of parts; non-stable time's parts are inherently ordered without need for a second time. Thus no regress.

Key Terms
الزمان (al-zamān): time
قار الذات (qārr al-dhāt): stable in essence
القبلية والبعدية (al-qabliyya wa al-baʿdiyya): priority and posteriority
التسلسل (al-tasalsul): infinite regress

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that succession necessarily requires a medium (another time) to measure the before and after.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that priority and posteriority are intrinsic to the parts of time; they need no external time.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's response, what is the basis for the priority and posteriority of parts of time?
A) Another time that measures them
B) The motion of the celestial sphere
C) Their own essences (bi-dhātayhimā)
D) The stable body in which they inhere

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the argument that time's existence leads to absurdities by showing that time's parts are inherently ordered and do not require a second time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument assumed that if time were stable, present and past would be identical, leading to absurdity. Razi corrected this by showing that stability does not erase difference between parts. In your spiritual life, do you ever assume that constancy in God's attributes means that your past mistakes are still present before Him? How does the intrinsic order of time encourage you to seek forgiveness and leave the past behind?
?
If time's parts are intrinsically ordered without needing a second time, then creation itself carries its own sequence from God. This week, when you feel anxious about the future, will you remember that the order of events is not arbitrary but woven into existence by the All-Wise? What is one worry you can surrender to that truth right now?
?
Razi's refutation required deep engagement with the argument rather than dismissing it. Think of a doubt or criticism of your faith that you have avoided examining closely. Will you sit with it this week, using the tools of reason that God gave you, trusting that careful analysis can strengthen your conviction? What specific step will you take to face that challenge?

The Dilemma of the 'Now'
Opponent enumerates the dilemma of the 'now' as divisible or indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that the 'now' leads to atomism or contradiction, and why it challenges the existence of time.

Core Idea
The 'now' cannot be a part of time without absurdity.

Before presenting his own view, Imam Razi outlines a sophisticated argument against the reality of time. The argument focuses on the present moment (al-ān) and forces a dilemma: either it is divisible, leading to non-existence of its parts, or it is indivisible, leading to atomistic time. This sets the stage for Razi's alternative conception.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note how it divides time into three categories (past, future, present) and focuses on the present moment 'now'.
This is a classic kalam argument against the reality of time.
2
Identify the dilemma
Recognize the two possibilities for the 'now': divisible or indivisible. See what consequences each leads to.
3
Understand the consequences
If divisible, parts don't coexist; if indivisible, non-existence is instantaneous, leading to a succession of 'nows' and atomistic composition of the body.
This shows why the argument is considered a problem for time's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن الزمان إما الماضي أو المستقبل أو الحال، ولا شك أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان، أما الحال فهو الآن، وهو إما أن يتكون منقسما أو لا يكون، فإن كان منقسما لم توجد أجزاؤه مما فلا يكون الذي فرضناه موجودا موجودا هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن منقسما كان عدمه دفعة لا محالة وعند فنائه يحدث أمر آخر دفعة فيلزم منه تعاقب الآنات، ويلزم منه تركيب الجسم من نقط متتابعة هذا خلف. أقول: الزمان أما الماضي وإما المستقبل وليس له قسم هو الآن إنما الآن فصل مشترك بين الماضي والمستقبل كالنقطة في الخط، والماضي الصرف ليس بمعدوم مطلقا إنما هو معدوم في المستقبل والمستقبل معدوم في الماضي وكلاهما في الآن وكل واحد منهما موجود في حده، وليس عدم شيء في شيء هو عدمه مطلقا، فإن السماء معدوم في البيت وليس المعدوم في موضعه، ولو كان الآن جزءا من الزمان لما أمكن قسمة الزمان إلى قسمين الغداة إلى الآن ومن الآن إلى العشاء، فإن كان الآن جزءا لم تكن القسمة صحيحة ولا أمكن قسمة مقدار من الزمان إلى قسمين، فالآن موجود وهو عرض حال في الزمان كالفصل المشترك في الخط وليس بجزء من الزمان وفنائه إلا تغير زمان فلا يلزم منه تنافي الآنات.
The second: that time is either the past, the future, or the present. There is no doubt that the past and future are non-existent. As for the present, it is the 'now' (al-ān). And it either is divisible or not. If it is divisible, then its parts do not exist together, so what we supposed to be existent would not be existent—this is a contradiction. If it is not divisible, then its non-existence occurs instantly inevitably, and upon its annihilation another thing occurs instantly, so the succession of 'nows' follows, and it follows that the body is composed of consecutive points—this is a contradiction. I say: Time is either past or future, and it has no part that is the 'now'. Rather, the 'now' is a common boundary (faṣl mushtarak) between the past and future, like the point on the line. The pure past is not absolutely non-existent; rather it is non-existent in the future, and the future is non-existent in the past, and both are in the 'now', and each one is existent in its own limit. The non-existence of a thing in something is not its absolute non-existence; for the sky is non-existent in the house, but it is not non-existent in its own place. If the 'now' were a part of time, then it would not be possible to divide time into two parts: from morning to the 'now' and from the 'now' to evening. For if the 'now' were a part, the division would not be correct, nor would it be possible to divide a quantity of time into two parts. Thus the 'now' is existent; it is an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in time like the common boundary on the line, and it is not a part of time. Its annihilation is only the change of time, so the incompatibility of 'nows' does not follow.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument against time based on the nature of 'now'. The argument says past and future are non-existent, and the present 'now' is either divisible (impossible) or indivisible (leading to atomism). Response: The 'now' is not a part of time; it is a boundary between past and future, like a point on a line. Past and future are not absolutely non-existent; they are existent in their own times. The 'now' is an accident inhering in time, not a discrete part. Thus no atomism.

Key Terms
الآن (al-ān): the now
الزمان (al-zamān): time

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the opponent's argument, what happens if the 'now' is divisible?
A) Its parts exist together
B) Its parts do not exist together, so it is not existent
C) It leads to atomism
D) It becomes a boundary

Key Takeaway
The opponent argues that the 'now' cannot be a part of time without leading to contradiction or atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the opponent's argument that the 'now' leads to contradiction, do you feel your own sense of the present moment is logically indefensible? How does that awareness affect your trust in rational analysis?
?
The opponent argues that if the 'now' is indivisible, it instantly vanishes—yet you experience a continuous flow of moments. What does this tension between logic and experience reveal about the limits of human reasoning?
?
If the opponent's argument were true and time is not real, how would that change the way you view your responsibilities and the weight of your actions in each 'now'? Will you live differently after grappling with this dilemma?

Razi's Refutation: The Now as a Boundary
Imam Razi redefines the now as a common boundary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's solution: the 'now' is not a part of time but a common boundary (faṣl mushtarak) between past and future, analogous to a point on a line, thus avoiding atomism.

Core Idea
The 'now' is a boundary, not a discrete part of time.

Imam Razi responds to the argument by redefining the nature of the 'now'. He argues that the 'now' is not a part of time but a common boundary between past and future, like a point dividing a line. This preserves the continuity of time and avoids the problems of divisibility and atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's response
Read the Arabic passage of Razi's response. Notice how he begins by redefining the categories of time, denying that the 'now' is a third part.
2
Understand the boundary concept
Grasp the analogy: the 'now' is like a point on a line—it is not a part of the line but a boundary between segments.
This is the core idea of the lesson.
3
Address the misconception about non-existence
Razi clarifies that past and future are not absolutely non-existent; they are non-existent in each other's domain but existent in their own limits.
4
See the implications
If the 'now' were a part, division of time would be impossible. Since division is possible (e.g., morning to now, now to evening), the 'now' must be a boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن الزمان إما الماضي أو المستقبل أو الحال، ولا شك أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان، أما الحال فهو الآن، وهو إما أن يتكون منقسما أو لا يكون، فإن كان منقسما لم توجد أجزاؤه مما فلا يكون الذي فرضناه موجودا موجودا هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن منقسما كان عدمه دفعة لا محالة وعند فنائه يحدث أمر آخر دفعة فيلزم منه تعاقب الآنات، ويلزم منه تركيب الجسم من نقط متتابعة هذا خلف. أقول: الزمان أما الماضي وإما المستقبل وليس له قسم هو الآن إنما الآن فصل مشترك بين الماضي والمستقبل كالنقطة في الخط، والماضي الصرف ليس بمعدوم مطلقا إنما هو معدوم في المستقبل والمستقبل معدوم في الماضي وكلاهما في الآن وكل واحد منهما موجود في حده، وليس عدم شيء في شيء هو عدمه مطلقا، فإن السماء معدوم في البيت وليس المعدوم في موضعه، ولو كان الآن جزءا من الزمان لما أمكن قسمة الزمان إلى قسمين الغداة إلى الآن ومن الآن إلى العشاء، فإن كان الآن جزءا لم تكن القسمة صحيحة ولا أمكن قسمة مقدار من الزمان إلى قسمين، فالآن موجود وهو عرض حال في الزمان كالفصل المشترك في الخط وليس بجزء من الزمان وفنائه إلا تغير زمان فلا يلزم منه تنافي الآنات.
The second: that time is either the past, the future, or the present. There is no doubt that the past and future are non-existent. As for the present, it is the 'now' (al-ān). And it either is divisible or not. If it is divisible, then its parts do not exist together, so what we supposed to be existent would not be existent—this is a contradiction. If it is not divisible, then its non-existence occurs instantly inevitably, and upon its annihilation another thing occurs instantly, so the succession of 'nows' follows, and it follows that the body is composed of consecutive points—this is a contradiction. I say: Time is either past or future, and it has no part that is the 'now'. Rather, the 'now' is a common boundary (faṣl mushtarak) between the past and future, like the point on the line. The pure past is not absolutely non-existent; rather it is non-existent in the future, and the future is non-existent in the past, and both are in the 'now', and each one is existent in its own limit. The non-existence of a thing in something is not its absolute non-existence; for the sky is non-existent in the house, but it is not non-existent in its own place. If the 'now' were a part of time, then it would not be possible to divide time into two parts: from morning to the 'now' and from the 'now' to evening. For if the 'now' were a part, the division would not be correct, nor would it be possible to divide a quantity of time into two parts. Thus the 'now' is existent; it is an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in time like the common boundary on the line, and it is not a part of time. Its annihilation is only the change of time, so the incompatibility of 'nows' does not follow.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument against time based on the nature of 'now'. The argument says past and future are non-existent, and the present 'now' is either divisible (impossible) or indivisible (leading to atomism). Response: The 'now' is not a part of time; it is a boundary between past and future, like a point on a line. Past and future are not absolutely non-existent; they are existent in their own times. The 'now' is an accident inhering in time, not a discrete part. Thus no atomism.

Key Terms
الآن (al-ān): the now
الفصل المشترك (al-faṣl al-mushtarak): common boundary
النقطة (al-nuqṭa): point
الزمان (al-zamān): time

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'now' is a minimal part of time that must be either divisible or indivisible, leading to absurdity.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the 'now' is not a part of time but a common boundary, like a point on a line, which avoids the dilemma.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the 'now' (al-ān)?
A) A part of time that is indivisible
B) A common boundary between past and future like a point on a line
C) A pure non-existence
D) A divisible substance

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi redefines the 'now' as a boundary (faṣl mushtarak) between past and future, preserving the continuity of time and avoiding atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the 'now' is a boundary, not a part of time. How does this understanding challenge the way you live in the present moment? Are you treating the 'now' as if it belongs to you, when it is merely a fleeting boundary between what you have done and what you will do? What does this teach you about the urgency of seizing the moment for Allah's sake?
?
If the 'now' is an accident that perishes with the change of time, what does that say about your attachment to this world? Are you holding onto moments as if they are permanent, when each 'now' is only a point of decision? How can this truth drive you to prioritize your actions for the Hereafter over momentary pleasures?
?
Razi uses the analogy of a point on a line: your life is a line, and the 'now' is the only point where you can act. Reflect: In this very 'now', are you fulfilling your purpose of worshiping Allah, or are you letting it pass without intention? Commit to one concrete action you can take in this 'now' to draw closer to Him.

Discrete Quantities Are Not Real Attributes
Imam Razi argues that discrete quantities are not real attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that discrete quantities like number and unity are not real existent attributes, and how he refutes the philosophers' argument for their reality.

Core Idea
Numbers are not extra realities; they are mental constructs from units.

This passage tackles a foundational question in ontology: are numbers real? Imam Razi argues that discrete quantities such as number and unity are not additional existent attributes. He provides a rigorous logical proof against the reality of unity and duality, and then addresses the philosophers' opposing view. By engaging with this argument, you will see how kalam thinkers used logical analysis to clarify the nature of mathematical concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation to get the full argument.
Notice the abrupt ending with 'ثبت'.
2
Identify the main claim and the infinite regress argument
The passage begins by stating that discrete quantities are not existent. Find the proof that if unity were an added attribute, it would lead to infinite regress.
3
Understand the duality argument
Examine why duality cannot be one attribute subsisting in two units—either it is wholly in each (impossible) or distributed (not one).
4
Analyze the philosophers' argument and Razi's response
The philosophers claim unity is additional because 'one' differs from 'ten' in oneness. Razi shows this leads to absurdity if unity were negative or positive.
The term 'البولية' is unclear but may be 'الوجودية'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the passage ends with 'ثبت' (thus it is established) and what that establishes about discrete quantities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكميات المنفصلة فليست أموراً وجودية لأنه لا معنى للعدد إلا مجموع الوحدات ، والوحدة لا يجوز أن تكون عفة وجودية زائدة على الذات، وإلا لكان كل واحد من أشخاص تلك الماهية أعنى ماهية الوحدة وحدة فيلزم التسلسل ، لأن الأثنينية لو كانت صفة واحدة وهى قائمة بالوحدتين فإما أن تكون بتمامها قائمة بكل واحدة من الوحدتين فيلزم قيام الواحد بالاثنين ويلزم أن يكون كل وحدة وحدها اثنين وهو محال ، وإن توزعت على الوحدتين كان القائم بكل واحدة من الوحدتين غير القائم بالأخرى فلم تكن الأثنينية صفة واحدة بل مجموع الأمرين، وإن جاز ذلك فلتجعل الأثنينية نفس تينك الوحدتين
وأما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على كون الوحدة صفة زائدة بأن الإنسان الواحد يخالف العشرة في مسمى الواحدية ويشاركها في مسمى الإنسانية فالواحدية صفة زائدة على الماهية وليست أمرا عدميا لأنها لو كانت عدميا لكانت الكثرة فالكثرة إن كانت عدمية كانت الوحدة عدم العدم فتكون البولية ، وإن كانت وجوية ولا معنى للكثرة إلا مجموع الوحدات، وإذا كانت الوحدة عدمية لزم أن يكون مجموع المدمات أمرا وجوديا وهو محال ثبت
As for discrete quantities, they are not existent things. For number has no meaning except the sum of units. Unity cannot be an existent attribute (صفة وجودية) additional to the essence. Otherwise, each individual of that quiddity—i.e., the quiddity of unity—would be a unity, leading to infinite regress. Because duality, if it were one attribute subsisting in two units, then either it subsists in its entirety in each of the two units, leading to the subsisting of one in two, and making each unit alone two—which is impossible—or it is distributed over the two units, then what subsists in each unit is different from what subsists in the other, so duality is not one attribute but a sum of two things. And if that is permissible, let duality be those very two units. As for the philosophers, they have argued that unity is an additional attribute because the one human differs from ten in the meaning of oneness while sharing in the meaning of humanity. So oneness is an additional attribute to the essence, and it is not a negative thing, because if it were negative, then multiplicity would be a negative, and if multiplicity is negative, then unity would be the negation of negation, thus being positive. But if multiplicity is positive, and multiplicity has no meaning except the sum of units, and if unity is negative, then the sum of nonexistents would be an existent thing, which is impossible. [Thus it is established].
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage discusses discrete quantities (الكميات المنفصلة), i.e., numbers. The author argues they are not real, existent attributes. Number is merely the sum of units, and unity itself cannot be an extra attribute added to essence. The proof: if unity were an added attribute, then each instance of the quiddity of unity would itself be a unity, causing infinite regress. For duality: if it were one attribute subsisting in two units, it would have to subsist in each entirely (making each both one and two) or be distributed (making it not one attribute). The philosophers' argument for unity as a positive attribute is then presented: the one human differs from ten in oneness but shares humanity; thus oneness is additional. If oneness were negative, multiplicity would also be negative, but then oneness would be double negation (positive). However, if unity is negative, the sum of nonexistents (multiplicity) would be existent, which is absurd. This establishes that unity is not a positive attribute.

Key Terms
الكميات المنفصلة
الوحدة
الأثنينية
العدد
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Numbers and unity are real, extra attributes existing in the world.
Correction: According to Imam Razi, discrete quantities are not existent things; number is merely the sum of units, and unity cannot be an additional attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion about discrete quantities (الكميات المنفصلة)?
A) They are real existent attributes.
B) They are not existent things; number is just the sum of units, and unity is not an extra attribute.
C) They are eternal and necessary.
D) They are purely imaginary with no basis.

Key Takeaway
Discrete quantities like number and unity are not real, extra attributes—they are mental constructs derived from units, and any attempt to make them real leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you count your blessings or your sins, do you treat the number itself as something real and weighty? How might this lesson free you from the illusion that quantity adds reality where it does not exist? What would change if you focused on the quality of each unit rather than the count?
?
The philosophers argued that 'one human' and 'ten humans' differ in oneness, so oneness must be an extra attribute. In your life, do you mistakenly infer that being one of many in a category makes your identity depend on a number? How does this lesson challenge you to see your unity before Allah as a simple fact, not a construct to be defended?
?
Razi shows that thinking of duality as a single attribute leads to logical impossibility. Are you carrying any dualistic assumptions about yourself—like thinking you have two selves, a good and a bad—as if they were real separate attributes? What would it mean to accept that you are one sinner before Allah, not a composite of multiple attributes? Spend five minutes in honest self-examination and then make a concrete commitment to stop dividing your identity.

The Threefold Division of Originated Things
Razi divides originated things into three categories and refutes the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's threefold division of originated things and the theological argument against the third category, along with Razi's critique of that argument.

Core Idea
Not all divisions are viable — the third category collapses under its own logic.

Imam Razi begins by classifying all originated things into three possible categories. After presenting the classification, he reports the majority view that the third category is invalid and then critiques their main argument. This passage teaches us how kalam thinkers reason about categories and the limits of negative attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: classification, denial, argument, refutation.
Pay attention to the key terms.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage lists three kinds of originated things. Find each one: body, accident, and a hypothetical third kind that is neither.
The third category is what the theologians reject.
3
Understand the argument against the third category
The majority argue that if such a thing existed, it would share a negative attribute with Allah, implying equality. Grasp why they think this is problematic.
4
Analyze Razi's refutation
Razi says the argument is weak because sharing negations does not necessitate similarity. He gives a counterexample: any two distinct things share the negation of everything else.
This is the key logical point.
5
Reflect on the lesson about negative attributes
Think about why this matters: negative attributes alone cannot establish similarity or difference in essence. This principle is important in kalam.
Recall the common misunderstanding mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحدث إما أن يكون متحيزا أو قائما بالمتحيز أو لا متحيز ولا قائم بالمتحيز والقسم الثالث قد أنكره الجمهور من المتكلمين . وأقوى مالهم فيه أنا لو فرضنا موجودا غير متحيز ولا حال فيه لسكان مساويا لذات الله تعالى فيه ، ويلزم من الاستواء فيه الاستواء فى تمام الماهية وهذا ضعيف . لأن الاشتراك فى السلوب لا يقتضى التماثل وإلا لزم تماثل المختلفات ، لأن كل مختلفين فلابد وأن يشتركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنهما .
The originated thing is either a body (mutahayyiz), or a thing subsisting in a body (qā'im bil-mutahayyiz), or neither a body nor a thing subsisting in a body. The third category has been denied by the majority of theologians. Their strongest argument for it is that if we assume an existent that is neither a body nor a state in a body, it would be equal to the essence of God Almighty in that [attribute], and equality in that entails equality in the complete essence. This is weak, because sharing in negations (sulūb) does not necessitate similarity; otherwise that would necessitate the similarity of different things, since any two different things must necessarily share in the negation of everything other than them.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a classification and a refutation. Razi first divides originated things (muḥdathāt) into three categories: (1) a body (something that occupies space), (2) an accident (something that subsists in a body), and (3) something that is neither a body nor an accident. The third category is rejected by most theologians. Their strongest argument is that if such an entity existed, it would share with God the attribute of being neither a body nor an accident. This shared negative attribute would imply equality in essence, which is absurd. Razi, however, criticizes this argument as weak: sharing a negative attribute does not require similarity, because any two distinct things necessarily share the negation of everything else. For example, a stone and a human both share the negation of being a bird, but they are not similar. Thus the argument is insufficient.

Key Terms
محدث
متحيز
قائم بالمتحيز
سلوب
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing a negative attribute (like 'neither body nor accident') between two entities implies they are similar in essence.
Correction: Razi shows that sharing a negation does not necessitate similarity. Any two distinct things share infinitely many negations (e.g., a stone and a human both are not a bird), yet they are not similar.

Checkpoint
What is the third category of originated things according to Razi's classification?
A) A body
B) An accident
C) Neither a body nor an accident
D) Both a body and an accident

Key Takeaway
Sharing a negative attribute does not imply similarity of essence; this principle is crucial in kalam for distinguishing between Allah and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah with negations—like 'He is not a body'—do you ever unconsciously assume that because you also are not a body (in some sense), you share something with Him? How does this lesson expose that false comfort and call you to a deeper awe of His utter uniqueness?
?
Think of a time you compared Allah to something in creation, even unintentionally. After learning that shared negations do not imply similarity, what specific analogy will you now reject, and how will you train your mind to affirm Allah's absolute incomparability beyond mere negations?
?
If another believer told you, 'Allah is not limited, and I am not limited in being finite—so we share something,' how would you use Razi's argument to correct them without being dismissive? What would you say to help them see the weakness?

Qualities Specific to Quantities: Solidity and Softness
Razi argues that qualities specific to quantities are non-existent and defines solidity and softness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that solidity and softness are not real qualities but relational concepts dependent on atomist composition and absence of resistance.

Core Idea
Solidity and softness are not real; they reduce to composition and lack of resistance.

In this passage, Razi tackles the ontological status of qualities like solidity and softness. He argues that these are not independent existents but merely relational notions that depend on the atomist theory of matter. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi makes a general claim then defines two specific qualities.
2
Identify Razi's general claim
Razi states that qualities specific to quantities are non-existent. His reason: whatever indicates the invalidity of their substrate indicates their invalidity.
3
Understand the definitions
Razi defines solidity as composition (ta'līf) based on the atomist view (jawhar fard), and softness as lack of resistance (ʿadam al-mumānaʿa).
These definitions show that these qualities are not positive but relational.
4
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: If the atomist substrate is invalid, then these qualities are also invalid. This ties into the larger kalam debate about the reality of sensible qualities.
This lesson establishes a key atomist claim about sensible qualities.

أما الكيفيات فالمختصة منها بالكميات غير موجودة ، لأن مادل على بطلان ما يقوم به يدل على بطلانها، أما الصلابة وهى عبارة عن التأليف بناء على القول بالجوهر الفرد وأما اللين فهو عبارة عن عدم الممانعة فيكون

As for softness (līn), it is an expression for lack of resistance (ʿadam al-mumānaʿa). Thus...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage deals with sensible qualities that are specific to quantities, such as solidity and softness. Razi argues that these qualities are not really existent. The reason is that if the substrate (the body composed of atoms) is invalid, then these qualities are also invalid. He defines solidity as composition (ta'līf) according to the atomist view, and softness as the absence of resistance. The argument seems to be that these are not positive qualities but merely relational concepts.

Geometric Figures as Qualities of Quantities: Razi’s Argument
Razi argues that lines, circles, etc. are qualities specific to quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi’s argument that geometric figures are real qualities specific to quantities, and his critique of the atomist reduction of qualities to solidity and composition.

Core Idea
Geometric figures are real qualities of quantities, not reducible to solidity or softness.

Razi presents a straightforward observation: we all recognize shapes like lines and circles, and they are distinct. He then shows that the atomist claim that only solidity and composition matter fails because water, flesh, and dust have composition without solidity. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the flow of Razi’s argument. Notice that he begins by asserting the obvious existence of geometric figures.
These figures are given as examples: straight line, curved line, circle, sphere, angle.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi states that geometric figures are ‘qualities specific to quantities.’ This is his central thesis. Understand what that means: shapes are real properties that only belong to things that have magnitude.
3
Understand the objection and counterexample
Razi targets the atomist view that solidity and composition are the same. He provides counterexamples: water has composition but no solidity; flesh and skin also lack solidity; and lack of resistance appears in dust, vapor, and smoke without actual softness.
This shows the atomist reduction is false because it cannot account for these cases.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this matter? If geometric figures are real qualities of quantities, then the atomist framework is too narrow. This supports the reality of abstract mathematical objects in the physical world.
Razi uses common experience to challenge a philosophical theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : لاشك في وجود الخط المستقيم والخط المنحنى والدائرة والكرة والزاوية ، وامتياز بعضها من بعض ، وليس ذلك إلا كيفيات مختصة بالكميات ، ولو كانت الصلابة والتأليف واحدا على رأى القائلين بالجوهر الفرد ، لكان الماء عندهم مؤلف من الجواهر الفردة إذ ليس فيه صلابة وكذلك لحوم الحيوانات وجلدها وعدم الممانعة توجد في مثل الغبار والبخار والدخان من غيرلين .
I say: There is no doubt about the existence of the straight line, the curved line, the circle, the sphere, and the angle, and the distinction of some from others. That is nothing but qualities specific to quantities. And if solidity and composition were one [and the same] according to the view of those who affirm the atom, then water, according to them, would be composed of atoms since there is no solidity in it, and similarly the flesh of animals and their skin; and lack of resistance exists in things like dust, vapor, and smoke without softness.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This is a further gloss by Razi objecting to the view that the only qualities specific to quantities are solidity and softness. He points out that geometric figures like lines, circles, spheres, and angles clearly exist and are distinct from each other. These are precisely qualities specific to quantities. He then argues that if solidity and composition were the same (i.e., if solidity equals composition), then water, which has no solidity, would still be composed of atoms. Similarly, flesh and skin do not have solidity. And lack of resistance (softness) is found in dust, vapor, and smoke without being soft. Thus the reduction is untenable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Geometric figures are not real qualities but merely mental constructs or accidental properties.
Correction: Razi insists that geometric figures exist and are distinct qualities specific to quantities, grounded in the physical world.

Checkpoint

C) The existence of sounds

Key Takeaway
Razi affirms the reality of geometric figures as distinct qualities of quantities, undermining atomist attempts to reduce all qualities to solidity and softness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often reduce the world to its physical components, ignoring distinct qualities like shape and arrangement. When you last looked at a leaf or a snowflake, did you see it as a mere collection of atoms, or as a unique pattern that declares its Creator's wisdom? Today, pause to recognize the real, distinct qualities around you and let them evoke gratitude.
?
Water has no solidity, yet it is composed—Razi uses this to show that reality is richer than reductionism. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes dismiss intangible qualities like hope, humility, or love as unreal because they are not 'solid'? Examine your heart: are you ignoring real, composed spiritual states because they lack worldly strength? Commit to nurturing them today.

Accidents and Sensible Qualities: Sight
Razi defines accidents and details sight-perceived sensible qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accidents are defined and how sensible qualities perceived by sight, specifically colors, light, and darkness, are classified and debated among different theological schools.

Core Idea
Accidents perceived by sight include colors and light, but not darkness, which is merely absence of light.

This lesson establishes the foundational concept of accidents as properties that can inhere in bodies. Razi then dives into the first category of sensible qualities—those perceived by sight—and unpacks the debates among philosophers and theologians regarding the nature of colors, light, and darkness. You'll see how empirical examples are used to argue that darkness is not a positive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Define an accident
Read the first sentence of the passage where Razi defines an accident and divides it into what can and cannot be predicated of non-living things. Understand that 'sensible' here refers to what is perceived by the senses.
2
Explore sight-perceived qualities
Read the section on sight, which covers colors, light, and darkness. Note the different opinions on primary colors and the nature of light.
Pay attention to the Ash'ari position on light as a condition for visibility.
3
Analyze the argument against positive darkness
Study the example of the night observers to understand why Razi concludes that darkness is the absence of light, not a positive attribute.
This is a key empirical argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحال في المتحيز فهو العرض وهو إما أن يجوز اتصاف غير الحى به أو لا يجوز ، والأول هو المحسوس بإحدى الحواس والأكوان . وأما المحسوس فمنها المحسوس بالبصر إحساسا أوليا وهو الألوان والأضواء أما الألوان فالقدماء قالوا الخالص هو السواد والبياض إنما يتخيل من اختلاط الهوى بالأجسام الصغار الشفافة كما في الثلج والزجاج المدقوق ، ومنهم من اعترف بالبياض كما فى بياض البيض المسلوق ، والمعتزلة قالوا الخالص هو السواد و البياض والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة . أما الضوء فقيل إنه جسم وهو خطأ لأن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة في كونها مضيئة ومظلمة وعند أبي على الضوء شرط وجود اللون ، وعندنا شرط صحة كونه مرئيا . أما الظلمة فنا من قطع بكونها ثبوتية والأقرب أنها عدم الضوء عما من شأنه أن يصير مضيئا لأن في الليل إذا جلس إنسان عند النار وآخر بعيدا عنها فالبعيد يرى من كان قريبا من النار ويرى الهواء المتوسط بينهما مضينا والقريب لا يرى البعيد ويرى ذلك الهواء مظلما، فلو كانت الظالمة صفة ثبوتية قائمة بالهواء لما اختلف الحال ، ومنها المحسوسة بالسمع وهى الأصوات والحروف وهى كيفيات إما عارضة للأصوات كالسين والشين أو حادثة في آخر زمان حبس النفس وأول زمان إطلاقه كالباء والطاء ، ومنه يظهر أن الحروف غير الصوت ، ومنها المحسوسات بالذوق وهى الحرافة والمرارة والملوحة والحلاوة والدسومة والحموضة والعفوصة والقبض والتفاهة . تنبيه : لاشك أن الحرافة تفعل تفريقا والعفوصة قبضا ، فالمدرك بحس الذوق كله طعم محض أو أمر مركب من الطعم ، ومن تفريق الحاسة هذا متوقف فيه، ومنها المحسوسة باالمس وهي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبه واليبوسة و الثقل والخفة والصلابة واللين
As for that which subsists in the body (al-ḥāl fī al-mutahayyiz), it is the accident (ʿaraḍ). It is either such that it is possible for a non-living thing to be described by it or not possible. The first is that which is perceived by one of the senses and the [spatial] states (akwān). As for the perceived, among them are those perceived by sight (al-baṣar) as a primary sensation: colors and lights. As for colors: the ancients said that the pure [colors] are black and white; white is imagined from the mixing of air with small transparent bodies, as in snow and crushed glass. Some of them acknowledged white as in the white of a boiled egg. The Mu'tazila said that the pure [colors] are black, white, red, yellow, and green. As for light: it was said that it is a body, but this is an error because bodies are equal in being bodies but differ in being luminous and dark. According to Abu 'Ali, light is a condition for the existence of color; according to us, it is a condition for the validity of being visible. As for darkness: among us [Ash'aris?] there are those who firmly assert that it is positive; the closer [view] is that it is the absence of light from that which is capable of being luminous. Because at night, if a person sits near a fire and another far from it, the far one sees the one near the fire and sees the intervening air as luminous, while the near one does not see the far one and sees that air as dark. If darkness were a positive attribute subsisting in the air, the situation would not differ. Among them are those perceived by hearing (al-samʿ): sounds (aṣwāt) and letters (ḥurūf). They are qualities that either accidentally attach to sounds, like sīn and shīn, or occur at the end of the time of holding the breath and the beginning of its release, like bā' and ṭā'. From this it appears that letters are not sound. Among them are those perceived by taste (al-dhawq): pungency, bitterness, saltiness, sweetness, oiliness, sourness, astringency, contraction, and insipidity. Note: There is no doubt that pungency causes separation and astringency causes contraction. What is perceived by the sense of taste is either pure taste or something composed of taste; whether [pungency] is a different sense is something to be examined. Among them are those perceived by touch (al-lams): heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, solidity, and softness.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a classification of accidents (aʿrāḍ) as sensible qualities. Razi first distinguishes accidents that can be predicated of non-living things from those that cannot. The first category includes what is perceived by the senses and spatial states (akwān). He then enumerates the objects of each sense: (1) Sight: colors and lights. For colors, he reports different opinions: ancients (some say only black and white are pure, with white arising from air mixing with transparent bodies; others accept white as independent). Mu'tazila posit five pure colors. Light: refutes the view that light is a body; mentions that according to Abu Ali (al-Jubba'i?) light is a condition for color existence, while Ash'aris say it is a condition for visibility. Darkness: most likely it is the absence of light, proven by the example of night observers. (2) Hearing: sounds and letters. Letters are either accidental to sounds (like sīn and shīn) or produced at breath release (like bā' and ṭā'). Letters are not identical to sound. (3) Taste: lists nine tastes and notes that pungency and astringency have specific effects. (4) Touch: lists eight qualities. This is a comprehensive categorization of sensible accidents.

محسوس
لون
ضوء
ظلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Darkness is a positive, existing attribute.
Correction: Darkness is the absence of light from that which is capable of being luminous, as demonstrated by the night example.

Checkpoint

A) A positive attribute
B) The absence of light from something capable of being luminous

Key Takeaway
Accidents are properties of bodies, and among sensible qualities, sight perceives colors and light, while darkness is not a positive quality but the absence of light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the colors of the world, do you realise they are accidents sustained by Allah each moment? How does this change your attachment to material beauty?
?
Razi argues that darkness is merely the absence of light, not a positive thing. Is there an area of your life where you mistake an absence (of faith, of gratitude) for a fixed darkness? What step will you take today to invite light back?
?

Sensible Qualities: Hearing
Razi classifies sounds and letters as hearing-perceived qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sounds and letters are classified as sensible qualities perceived by hearing, and how letters are distinct from sounds.

Core Idea
Sounds and letters are qualities perceived by hearing, and letters are not identical to sound.

Razi continues his classification of sensible qualities with those perceived by hearing. He distinguishes between sounds and letters, explaining that some letters are accidental to sounds while others are produced by breath control. This lesson clarifies the Ash'ari position on the nature of letters and their relation to sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify hearing-perceived qualities
Read the passage that begins with 'منها المحسوسة بالسمع' and note that it includes sounds (aṣwāt) and letters (ḥurūf).
2
Distinguish letters from sounds
Pay attention to the two types of letters: those that accidentally attach to sounds (like sīn and shīn) and those that occur at the end of holding breath (like bā' and ṭā'). Understand why letters are not sound.
This distinction is important in kalam discussions on the createdness of the Quran.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحال في المتحيز فهو العرض وهو إما أن يجوز اتصاف غير الحى به أو لا يجوز ، والأول هو المحسوس بإحدى الحواس والأكوان . وأما المحسوس فمنها المحسوس بالبصر إحساسا أوليا وهو الألوان والأضواء أما الألوان فالقدماء قالوا الخالص هو السواد والبياض إنما يتخيل من اختلاط الهوى بالأجسام الصغار الشفافة كما في الثلج والزجاج المدقوق ، ومنهم من اعترف بالبياض كما فى بياض البيض المسلوق ، والمعتزلة قالوا الخالص هو السواد و البياض والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة . أما الضوء فقيل إنه جسم وهو خطأ لأن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة في كونها مضيئة ومظلمة وعند أبي على الضوء شرط وجود اللون ، وعندنا شرط صحة كونه مرئيا . أما الظلمة فنا من قطع بكونها ثبوتية والأقرب أنها عدم الضوء عما من شأنه أن يصير مضيئا لأن في الليل إذا جلس إنسان عند النار وآخر بعيدا عنها فالبعيد يرى من كان قريبا من النار ويرى الهواء المتوسط بينهما مضينا والقريب لا يرى البعيد ويرى ذلك الهواء مظلما، فلو كانت الظالمة صفة ثبوتية قائمة بالهواء لما اختلف الحال ، ومنها المحسوسة بالسمع وهى الأصوات والحروف وهى كيفيات إما عارضة للأصوات كالسين والشين أو حادثة في آخر زمان حبس النفس وأول زمان إطلاقه كالباء والطاء ، ومنه يظهر أن الحروف غير الصوت ، ومنها المحسوسات بالذوق وهى الحرافة والمرارة والملوحة والحلاوة والدسومة والحموضة والعفوصة والقبض والتفاهة . تنبيه : لاشك أن الحرافة تفعل تفريقا والعفوصة قبضا ، فالمدرك بحس الذوق كله طعم محض أو أمر مركب من الطعم ، ومن تفريق الحاسة هذا متوقف فيه، ومنها المحسوسة باالمس وهي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبه واليبوسة و الثقل والخفة والصلابة واللين
As for that which subsists in the body (al-ḥāl fī al-mutahayyiz), it is the accident (ʿaraḍ). It is either such that it is possible for a non-living thing to be described by it or not possible. The first is that which is perceived by one of the senses and the [spatial] states (akwān). As for the perceived, among them are those perceived by sight (al-baṣar) as a primary sensation: colors and lights. As for colors: the ancients said that the pure [colors] are black and white; white is imagined from the mixing of air with small transparent bodies, as in snow and crushed glass. Some of them acknowledged white as in the white of a boiled egg. The Mu'tazila said that the pure [colors] are black, white, red, yellow, and green. As for light: it was said that it is a body, but this is an error because bodies are equal in being bodies but differ in being luminous and dark. According to Abu 'Ali, light is a condition for the existence of color; according to us, it is a condition for the validity of being visible. As for darkness: among us [Ash'aris?] there are those who firmly assert that it is positive; the closer [view] is that it is the absence of light from that which is capable of being luminous. Because at night, if a person sits near a fire and another far from it, the far one sees the one near the fire and sees the intervening air as luminous, while the near one does not see the far one and sees that air as dark. If darkness were a positive attribute subsisting in the air, the situation would not differ. Among them are those perceived by hearing (al-samʿ): sounds (aṣwāt) and letters (ḥurūf). They are qualities that either accidentally attach to sounds, like sīn and shīn, or occur at the end of the time of holding the breath and the beginning of its release, like bā' and ṭā'. From this it appears that letters are not sound. Among them are those perceived by taste (al-dhawq): pungency, bitterness, saltiness, sweetness, oiliness, sourness, astringency, contraction, and insipidity. Note: There is no doubt that pungency causes separation and astringency causes contraction. What is perceived by the sense of taste is either pure taste or something composed of taste; whether [pungency] is a different sense is something to be examined. Among them are those perceived by touch (al-lams): heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, solidity, and softness.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a classification of accidents (aʿrāḍ) as sensible qualities. Razi first distinguishes accidents that can be predicated of non-living things from those that cannot. The first category includes what is perceived by the senses and spatial states (akwān). He then enumerates the objects of each sense: (1) Sight: colors and lights. For colors, he reports different opinions: ancients (some say only black and white are pure, with white arising from air mixing with transparent bodies; others accept white as independent). Mu'tazila posit five pure colors. Light: refutes the view that light is a body; mentions that according to Abu Ali (al-Jubba'i?) light is a condition for color existence, while Ash'aris say it is a condition for visibility. Darkness: most likely it is the absence of light, proven by the example of night observers. (2) Hearing: sounds and letters. Letters are either accidental to sounds (like sīn and shīn) or produced at breath release (like bā' and ṭā'). Letters are not identical to sound. (3) Taste: lists nine tastes and notes that pungency and astringency have specific effects. (4) Touch: lists eight qualities. This is a comprehensive categorization of sensible accidents.

Key Terms
صوت
حرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a type of letter according to Razi?
A) Letters accidentally attached to sounds
B) Letters occurring at the end of holding breath
C) Letters that are identical to sound
D) Letters like sīn and shīn

Key Takeaway
Hearing perceives sounds and letters, but letters are not the same as sounds; they are either accidental qualities or produced by breath.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone speak, do you distinguish between the sound itself and the letters that form words? How might this distinction change your appreciation of the speech of others, especially the recitation of the Quran? Will you now listen more carefully to the 'letters' as distinct qualities?
?
The scholars of kalam devoted effort to classifying even the simplest sensory experiences. Does your own mind ever pause to reflect on the building blocks of what you perceive—like sounds and letters—or do you take them for granted? What might this teach you about the depth of Allah's creation?

Sensible Qualities: Taste and Touch
Razi enumerates taste and touch qualities with a note on effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of sensible qualities perceived by taste and touch, including the different tastes and tactile qualities, and the note about pungency and astringency.

Core Idea
Taste and touch perceive distinct sets of qualities: taste includes nine flavors, touch includes eight tactile properties.

Razi completes his classification of sensible qualities with those perceived by taste and touch. He lists nine tastes and notes that some have specific effects. For touch, he lists eight qualities. This lesson provides a full inventory of the physical qualities that, according to kalam, can inhere in bodies and be perceived by the senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the taste qualities
Read the list of nine tastes: pungency, bitterness, saltiness, sweetness, oiliness, sourness, astringency, contraction, and insipidity. Note the remark about pungency causing separation.
2
Read the touch qualities
Read the list of eight tactile qualities: heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, solidity, and softness.
These are standard in kalam physics.
3
Understand the note
Consider the note that pungency causes separation and astringency causes contraction, and that all taste is either pure taste or something composed of taste.
This shows that kalam is concerned with the effects of qualities.

Definition of Accident and Its Divisions
Defines accident and introduces its two main divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of accident ('arad) and its two main categories: attributes that can belong to non-living things (sensible qualities and spatial states) and those that cannot.

Core Idea
An accident is a state that subsists in a body; it is either perceptible or not.

This passage begins by defining accident ('arad) as the state that subsists in an extended entity (mutahayyiz). It then divides accidents into two categories: those that can be attributed to non-living things (the sensible qualities and spatial states) and those that cannot. This foundational classification sets the stage for the detailed discussion of each sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage: 'وأما الحال في المتحيز فهو العرض'. Notice how the author defines accident as a state in an extended thing.
2
Identify the two divisions
Look for the phrase 'إما أن يجوز اتصاف غير الحى به أو لا يجوز'. This splits accidents into those that can be attributed to non-living things and those that cannot. The first group includes sensible qualities and 'akwan (spatial states like rest and motion).
3
Understand the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it separates attributes that are observable from those that are not, forming the basis for discussions about the nature of physical objects.
This connects to the learning objective.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحال في المتحيز فهو العرض وهو إما أن يجوز اتصاف غير الحى به أو لا يجوز ، والأول هو المحسوس بإحدى الحواس والأكوان . وأما المحسوس فمنها المحسوس بالبصر إحساسا أوليا وهو الألوان والأضواء أما الألوان فالقدماء قالوا الخالص هو السواد والبياض إنما يتخيل من اختلاط الهوى بالأجسام الصغار الشفافة كما في الثلج والزجاج المدقوق ، ومنهم من اعترف بالبياض كما فى بياض البيض المسلوق ، والمعتزلة قالوا الخالص هو السواد و البياض والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة . أما الضوء فقيل إنه جسم وهو خطأ لأن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة في كونها مضيئة ومظلمة وعند أبي على الضوء شرط وجود اللون ، وعندنا شرط صحة كونه مرئيا . أما الظلمة فنا من قطع بكونها ثبوتية والأقرب أنها عدم الضوء عما من شأنه أن يصير مضيئا لأن في الليل إذا جلس إنسان عند النار وآخر بعيدا عنها فالبعيد يرى من كان قريبا من النار ويرى الهواء المتوسط بينهما مضينا والقريب لا يرى البعيد ويرى ذلك الهواء مظلما، فلو كانت الظالمة صفة ثبوتية قائمة بالهواء لما اختلف الحال ، ومنها المحسوسة بالسمع وهى الأصوات والحروف وهى كيفيات إما عارضة للأصوات كالسين والشين أو حادثة في آخر زمان حبس النفس وأول زمان إطلاقه كالباء والطاء ، ومنه يظهر أن الحروف غير الصوت ، ومنها المحسوسات بالذوق وهى الحرافة والمرارة والملوحة والحلاوة والدسومة والحموضة والعفوصة والقبض والتفاهة . تنبيه : لاشك أن الحرافة تفعل تفريقا والعفوصة قبضا ، فالمدرك بحس الذوق كله طعم محض أو أمر مركب من الطعم ، ومن تفريق الحاسة هذا متوقف فيه، ومنها المحسوسة باالمس وهي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبه واليبوسة و الثقل والخفة والصلابة واللين
As for the state in the extended (mutahayyiz), it is the accident ('arad). Either it is permissible for a non-living thing to be described by it, or not. The first is what is perceived by one of the senses and the 'akwan (spatial states). As for the perceived: among them is what is perceived by sight as a primary sensation, and they are colors and lights. As for colors, the ancients said that the pure ones are black and white; whiteness is imagined from the mixture of air with small transparent bodies, as in snow and crushed glass. And among them are those who acknowledged whiteness as in the whiteness of boiled egg white. The Mu'tazila said that the pure are black, white, red, yellow, and green. As for light, it was said that it is a body, which is wrong because bodies are equal in corporeity and differ in being luminous and dark. According to Abu 'Ali, light is a condition for the existence of color; according to us, it is a condition for the validity of its being visible. As for darkness, some are certain that it is positive (thubutiyya); the closest is that it is the absence of light from that which is apt to become luminous, because in the night, if a person sits near a fire and another far from it, the far one sees the one near the fire and sees the intermediate air as luminous, while the near one does not see the far one and sees that air as dark. If darkness were a positive attribute subsisting in the air, the situation would not differ. Among them are what is perceived by hearing: sounds and letters, which are qualities. Either they are accidental to sounds, like sin and shin, or they occur at the final time of holding the breath and the beginning of releasing it, like ba' and ta'. From this it appears that letters are not sound. Among them are what is perceived by taste: pungency, bitterness, saltiness, sweetness, oiliness, sourness, astringency, constriction, and insipidity. Note: There is no doubt that pungency causes separation and astringency causes constriction. So what is perceived by the sense of taste is either pure taste or a composite of taste. And the division of this sense is disputed. Among them are what is perceived by touch: heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, hardness, and softness.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage begins by defining accident ('arad) as that which subsists in a bodily entity (mutahayyiz). It then divides accidents into those that can be attributed to non-living things, which are the perceptible qualities (mahsusat) and 'akwan (states of rest, motion, etc.). The author proceeds to classify the perceptible qualities by sense. Under sight, he addresses colors and light. He reports the views of the ancients and the Mu'tazila on pure primary colors, and then discusses light, rejecting the idea that light is a body. He presents Abu 'Ali's view that light is a condition for the existence of color and the Ash'ari position that it is a condition for visibility. Regarding darkness, he argues it is the privation of light, not a positive attribute, using an example of two people near a fire. Under hearing, he distinguishes sounds from letters, explaining that letters are either accidental qualities of sounds or produced at moments of breath release. Under taste, he lists nine basic tastes and adds a note that pungency and astringency have distinct effects. Under touch, he lists eight qualities: heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, hardness, and softness.

Key Terms
العرض
المحسوس
الأكوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That accidents cannot be categorized or that all accidents are sensible.
Correction: The author shows that accidents have two types: those that can belong to non-living things (sensible and spatial) and those that cannot (e.g., psychological attributes).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is included in the first division of accidents?
A) Knowledge
B) Sensible qualities
C) Life
D) Power

Key Takeaway
Accident is a state in a body, and its primary division is into attributes that can and cannot belong to non-living things, with sensible qualities and spatial states forming the first category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You encounter countless accidents every day—colors, sounds, movements, and more. The passage teaches that some accidents can exist in non-living things and some cannot. Pause and identify one accident from each category in your current environment. How does this awareness change your sense of the contingency of all created things?
?
This lesson distinguishes between accidents that belong to non-living things and those that belong only to living beings. Consider your own thoughts: they are accidents that cannot reside in a rock. How does this truth call you to gratitude for the life and intellect Allah has given you? What will you do today to use that gift in a way that pleases Him?

Sensible Qualities of Sight: Colors, Light, and Darkness
Classifies visual qualities and argues that darkness is privation of light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of visible qualities into colors and lights, and the argument that darkness is the absence of light, not a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Colors and light are the primary visual qualities; darkness is merely the absence of light.

After establishing the general framework, the author delves into the sensible qualities perceived by sight. He discusses the views of ancient philosophers and the Mu'tazila on pure colors, then examines light and darkness, providing a reasoned argument that darkness is not a positive attribute but the privation of light. This lesson focuses on that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sight
Read the section from 'وأما المحسوس فمنها المحسوس بالبصر' to 'لاختلف الحال'. Note the structure: first colors, then light, then darkness.
2
Compare views on pure colors
Identify the ancients' view (only black and white are pure) and the Mu'tazila's view (five pure colors). Notice the example of snow and crushed glass.
3
Understand the argument on darkness
Read the example of two people near a fire. The author uses this to show that darkness cannot be a positive attribute because perception would not differ if it were. Conclude that darkness is the absence of light.
This is the core argument.

وأما الحال في المتحيز فهو العرض وهو إما أن يجوز اتصاف غير الحى به أو لا يجوز ، والأول هو المحسوس بإحدى الحواس والأكوان . وأما المحسوس فمنها المحسوس بالبصر إحساسا أوليا وهو الألوان والأضواء أما الألوان فالقدماء قالوا الخالص هو السواد والبياض إنما يتخيل من اختلاط الهوى بالأجسام الصغار الشفافة كما في الثلج والزجاج المدقوق ، ومنهم من اعترف بالبياض كما فى بياض البيض المسلوق ، والمعتزلة قالوا الخالص هو السواد و البياض والحمرة والصفرة والخضرة . أما الضوء فقيل إنه جسم وهو خطأ لأن الأجسام متساوية في الجسمية ومختلفة في كونها مضيئة ومظلمة وعند أبي على الضوء شرط وجود اللون ، وعندنا شرط صحة كونه مرئيا . أما الظلمة فنا من قطع بكونها ثبوتية والأقرب أنها عدم الضوء عما من شأنه أن يصير مضيئا لأن في الليل إذا جلس إنسان عند النار وآخر بعيدا عنها فالبعيد يرى من كان قريبا من النار ويرى الهواء المتوسط بينهما مضينا والقريب لا يرى البعيد ويرى ذلك الهواء مظلما، فلو كانت الظالمة صفة ثبوتية قائمة بالهواء لما اختلف الحال ، ومنها المحسوسة بالسمع وهى الأصوات والحروف وهى كيفيات إما عارضة للأصوات كالسين والشين أو حادثة في آخر زمان حبس النفس وأول زمان إطلاقه كالباء والطاء ، ومنه يظهر أن الحروف غير الصوت ، ومنها المحسوسات بالذوق وهى الحرافة والمرارة والملوحة والحلاوة والدسومة والحموضة والعفوصة والقبض والتفاهة . تنبيه : لاشك أن الحرافة تفعل تفريقا والعفوصة قبضا ، فالمدرك بحس الذوق كله طعم محض أو أمر مركب من الطعم ، ومن تفريق الحاسة هذا متوقف فيه، ومنها المحسوسة باالمس وهي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبه واليبوسة و الثقل والخفة والصلابة واللين
As for the state in the extended (mutahayyiz), it is the accident ('arad). Either it is permissible for a non-living thing to be described by it, or not. The first is what is perceived by one of the senses and the 'akwan (spatial states). As for the perceived: among them is what is perceived by sight as a primary sensation, and they are colors and lights. As for colors, the ancients said that the pure ones are black and white; whiteness is imagined from the mixture of air with small transparent bodies, as in snow and crushed glass. And among them are those who acknowledged whiteness as in the whiteness of boiled egg white. The Mu'tazila said that the pure are black, white, red, yellow, and green. As for light, it was said that it is a body, which is wrong because bodies are equal in corporeity and differ in being luminous and dark. According to Abu 'Ali, light is a condition for the existence of color; according to us, it is a condition for the validity of its being visible. As for darkness, some are certain that it is positive (thubutiyya); the closest is that it is the absence of light from that which is apt to become luminous, because in the night, if a person sits near a fire and another far from it, the far one sees the one near the fire and sees the intermediate air as luminous, while the near one does not see the far one and sees that air as dark. If darkness were a positive attribute subsisting in the air, the situation would not differ. Among them are what is perceived by hearing: sounds and letters, which are qualities. Either they are accidental to sounds, like sin and shin, or they occur at the final time of holding the breath and the beginning of releasing it, like ba' and ta'. From this it appears that letters are not sound. Among them are what is perceived by taste: pungency, bitterness, saltiness, sweetness, oiliness, sourness, astringency, constriction, and insipidity. Note: There is no doubt that pungency causes separation and astringency causes constriction. So what is perceived by the sense of taste is either pure taste or a composite of taste. And the division of this sense is disputed. Among them are what is perceived by touch: heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, hardness, and softness.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage begins by defining accident ('arad) as that which subsists in a bodily entity (mutahayyiz). It then divides accidents into those that can be attributed to non-living things, which are the perceptible qualities (mahsusat) and 'akwan (states of rest, motion, etc.). The author proceeds to classify the perceptible qualities by sense. Under sight, he addresses colors and light. He reports the views of the ancients and the Mu'tazila on pure primary colors, and then discusses light, rejecting the idea that light is a body. He presents Abu 'Ali's view that light is a condition for the existence of color and the Ash'ari position that it is a condition for visibility. Regarding darkness, he argues it is the privation of light, not a positive attribute, using an example of two people near a fire. Under hearing, he distinguishes sounds from letters, explaining that letters are either accidental qualities of sounds or produced at moments of breath release. Under taste, he lists nine basic tastes and adds a note that pungency and astringency have distinct effects. Under touch, he lists eight qualities: heat, cold, moisture, dryness, heaviness, lightness, hardness, and softness.

الأضواء
الظلمة
السواد
البياض

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That darkness is a positive attribute or a body.
Correction: The author argues darkness is the absence of light, not a positive quality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is darkness?
A) A positive attribute subsisting in air
B) A kind of body
C) The absence of light from something apt to be luminous
D) A mixture of colors

Key Takeaway
Colors and lights are the primary visual qualities; darkness is not a positive attribute but the absence of light, as shown by the varying perception of air near a fire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you mistook the absence of something for a positive quality—perhaps an absence of conflict for peace, or absence of doubt for faith. How does this lesson’s argument about darkness challenge you to re-examine that perception? What would change if you saw it as privation rather than a thing?
?
The passage shows that what we perceive as dark depends on our position relative to light. Where in your spiritual life might you be too close to a 'fire'—to comfort, routine, or arrogance—so that you perceive darkness where others see light? What would it take to step back and see truth?

Sensible Qualities of Hearing, Taste, and Touch
Enumerates sensible qualities for hearing, taste, and touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of sensible qualities for hearing (sounds and letters), taste (nine flavors), and touch (eight tactile qualities).

Core Idea
Hearing perceives sounds and letters; taste perceives distinct flavors; touch perceives pairs of opposite qualities.

After discussing sight, the author turns to the other senses. He explains that hearing perceives sounds and letters, clarifying that letters are not sounds but qualities that arise at specific moments. For taste, he lists nine distinct tastes and notes that some involve both taste and a tactile effect. For touch, he enumerates eight qualities, which are often considered as four pairs of opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the hearing section
Read from 'ومنها المحسوسة بالسمع' to 'أن الحروف غير الصوت'. Note the distinction between sounds and letters, and the two types of letters.
2
Read the taste section
From 'ومنها المحسوسات بالذوق' to 'متوقف فيه'. Count the nine tastes and notice the note about pungency and astringency.
3
Read the touch section
Read 'ومنها المحسوسة باالمس وهي الحرارة والبرودة...' to the end. Identify the eight tactile qualities.
Note that these are often paired: hot-cold, wet-dry, heavy-light, hard-soft.

Coldness Is Not the Absence of Heat
Razi refutes the claim that coldness is privation of heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why coldness cannot be merely the privation of heat and why it must be a positive, perceptible quality.

Core Idea
We sense cold directly, and absence cannot be sensed.

Some philosophers argued that coldness is simply the lack of heat. But if that were true, we wouldn't be able to sense cold—yet we clearly do. Imam al-Razi presents a crisp refutation grounded in the nature of sensation and absence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the author states the claim and then the refutation.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the claim and the refutation
The claim is that coldness is the absence of heat. The refutation is based on sensation: we perceive a specific quality from cold things, and absence cannot be perceived.
3
Understand the logical flaw
If coldness were just absence of heat, then when we sense a hot body, we would also sense coldness (since heat is present, its absence would be there too). This is absurd, so the premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : منهم من جعل البرودة عدم الحرارة) خطأ لأنا نحس من البارد بكيفية مخصوصة فذلك المحوس ليس عدم الحرارة لأن العدم لا يحس به ولا الجسم وإلا لكان الإحساس بالجسم حال حرارته إحساسا بالبرودة
Question: Some of them made coldness the absence of heat. It is wrong because we sense from the cold a specific quality, so that sensed thing is not the absence of heat, because absence is not sensed, nor is the body, otherwise the sensation of the body while it is hot would be a sensation of coldness.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer unit. It begins by stating a claim: some philosophers held that coldness is nothing but the privation of heat. The author rejects this, arguing that we directly perceive a specific quality from cold objects. That which is sensed cannot be a mere absence (Adam) because absence is not perceptible. If coldness were simply absence of heat, then when we sense a hot body, we would also be sensing coldness, which is absurd. Thus coldness must be a positive attribute.

Key Terms
البرودة
الحرارة
العدم
الإحساس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Coldness is merely the absence of heat.
Correction: Coldness is a positive, sensed quality, not a mere absence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the view that coldness is the absence of heat?
A) Because coldness is a body.
B) Because absence can never be sensed.
C) Because heat is more fundamental.
D) Because cold objects have no qualities.

Key Takeaway
Coldness is a positive quality because it is directly sensed, and absence cannot be sensed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually 'cold' or distant from Allah, do you treat it as merely the absence of spiritual warmth, or as a positive test with a specific quality? How does Razi's logic—that what you sense cannot be mere absence—push you to ask what positive message Allah is sending you through that feeling? What step will you take today to seek that message?
?
Think of a person you've described as 'not mean' or 'not bad.' According to this lesson, reducing a quality to a negation is flawed. What positive quality does that person actually have? How can you affirm it as a real attribute, and how might that change your gratitude to Allah for creating them that way?
?
The philosophers reduced coldness to absence of heat. What is one belief you hold about Allah or His creation that you have reduced to a 'lack' (e.g., 'Allah is not unjust' instead of 'Allah is perfectly just')? How does Razi's method of insisting on positive, sensed qualities challenge you to adjust that belief today?
